</table>
<p>The <b>Safavid dynasty</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa" xml:lang="fa">دودمان صفوی</span>‎‎ <i>Dudmān e Safavi</i>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="az" xml:lang="az">صفويلر سولاله‌سى</span>‎ <span lang="az" xml:lang="az"><i>Səfəvilər sülaləsi</i></span>) was one of the most significant ruling dynasties of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, often considered the beginning of modern Iranian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The Safavid <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shahs</a> ruled over one of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_Empires" title="Gunpowder Empires">gunpowder empires</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> They ruled one of the greatest Iranian empires after the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Iran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and established the <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a> as the official religion of the empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> marking one of the most important turning points in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_history" title="Muslim history">Muslim history</a>.</p>
<p>The Safavid dynasty had its origin in the <a href="/wiki/Safaviyya" title="Safaviyya">Safaviyya</a> <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi order</a>, which was established in the city of <a href="/wiki/Ardabil" title="Ardabil">Ardabil</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan_(Iran)" title="Azerbaijan (Iran)">Azerbaijan</a> region. It was of mixed ancestry (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.M._27-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.M.-27">[27]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_people" title="Azerbaijani people">Azerbaijani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fryeiranica_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-fryeiranica-28">[28]</a></sup> which included intermarriages with <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYarshater2001493_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYarshater2001493-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhanbaghi2006130_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhanbaghi2006130-31">[31]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greek</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anthony_Bryer_1975_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anthony_Bryer_1975-32">[32]</a></sup> dignitaries). From their base in Ardabil, the Safavids established control over parts of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> and reasserted the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Iranian identity</a> of the region,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> thus becoming the first native dynasty since the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> to establish a unified Iranian state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Safavids ruled from 1501 to 1722 (experiencing a brief restoration from 1729 to 1736) and, at their height, they controlled all of modern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, as well as parts of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>.</p>
<p>Despite their demise in 1736, the legacy that they left behind was the revival of Persia as an economic stronghold between East and <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>, the establishment of an efficient state and <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a> based upon "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Checks_and_balances" title="Checks and balances">checks and balances</a>", their architectural innovations and their patronage for <a href="/wiki/Safavid_art" title="Safavid art">fine arts</a>. The Safavids have also left their mark down to the present era by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_from_Sunnism_to_Shiism" title="Safavid conversion of Iran from Sunnism to Shiism">spreading Shi'a Islam</a> in Iran, as well as major parts of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genealogy.E2.80.94ancestors_of_the_Safavids_and_its_multi-cultural_identity">Genealogy—ancestors of the Safavids and its multi-cultural identity</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty_family_tree" title="Safavid dynasty family tree">Safavid dynasty family tree</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safaviyya" title="Safaviyya">Safaviyya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safvat_as-safa" title="Safvat as-safa">Safvat as-safa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silsilat-al-nasab-i_Safaviya" title="Silsilat-al-nasab-i Safaviya">Silsilat-al-nasab-i Safaviya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firuz_Shah_Zarin-Kolah" title="Firuz Shah Zarin-Kolah">Firuz Shah Zarin-Kolah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_mothers_of_the_Safavid_Shahs" title="List of the mothers of the Safavid Shahs">List of the mothers of the Safavid Shahs</a></div>
<p><span id="Genealogy"></span>The Safavid Kings themselves claimed to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seyyed" title="Seyyed">Seyyeds</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> family descendants of the Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, although many scholars have cast doubt on this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> There seems now to be a consensus among scholars that the Safavid family hailed from Persian Kurdistan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> and later moved to Azerbaijan, finally settling in the 11th century CE at <a href="/wiki/Ardabil" title="Ardabil">Ardabil</a>. Traditional pre-1501 Safavid manuscripts trace the lineage of the Safavids to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_peoples" title="Kurdish peoples">Kurdish</a> dignitary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firuz_Shah_Zarin-Kulah" title="Firuz Shah Zarin-Kulah">Firuz Shah Zarin-Kulah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.M._27-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.M.-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to some historians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csatoetal_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-csatoetal-39">[39]</a></sup> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Frye" title="Richard Frye">Richard Frye</a>, the Safavids were of Turkicized Iranian origin:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fryeiranica_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-fryeiranica-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Turkish speakers of Azerbaijan are mainly descended from the earlier Iranian speakers, several pockets of whom still exist in the region. A massive migration of Oghuz Turks in the 11th and 12th centuries not only Turkified Azerbaijan but also Anatolia. The Azeri Turks are Shiʿites and were founders of the Safavid dynasty.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Other historians, such as <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Minorsky" title="Vladimir Minorsky">Vladimir Minorsky</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Savory" title="Roger Savory">Roger Savory</a>, support this idea:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>From the evidence available at the present time, it is certain that the Safavid family was of indigenous Iranian stock, and not of Turkish ancestry as it is sometimes claimed. It is probable that the family originated in Persian Kurdistan, and later moved to Azerbaijan, where they adopted the Azari form of Turkish spoken there, and eventually settled in the small town of Ardabil sometimes during the eleventh century.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>By the time of the establishment of the Safavid empire, the members of the family were native Turkish-speaking and Turkicized,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savory07_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-savory07-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and some of the Shahs composed poems in their native Turkish language. Concurrently, the Shahs themselves also supported Persian literature, poetry and art projects including the grand <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh_of_Shah_Tahmasp" title="Shahnameh of Shah Tahmasp">Shahnameh</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Shah Tahmasp</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> while members of the family and some Shahs composed Persian poetry as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The authority of the Safavids was religiously based, and their claim to legitimacy was founded on being direct male descendants of the Ali,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> the cousin and son-in-law of Muhammad, and regarded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27ite" title="Shi'ite">Shi'ites</a> as the first Imam.</p>
<p>Furthermore, the dynasty was from the very start thoroughly intermarried with both <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greek</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In addition, from the official establishment of the dynasty in 1501, the dynasty would continue to have many intermarriages with both <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a> as well as again Georgian dignitaries, especially with the advent of king <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Tahmasp I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYarshater2001493_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYarshater2001493-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhanbaghi2006130_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhanbaghi2006130-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background.E2.80.94The_Safavid_Sufi_Order">Background—The Safavid Sufi Order</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Safaviyya" title="Safaviyya">Safaviyya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safi_al-Din_Ardabili" title="Safi al-Din Ardabili">Safi al-Din Ardabili</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ideology_of_Safavids" title="Ideology of Safavids">Ideology of Safavids</a></div>
<p>Safavid history begins with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Safaviyya" title="Safaviyya">Safaviyya</a> by its eponymous founder <a href="/wiki/Safi-ad-din_Ardabili" title="Safi-ad-din Ardabili">Safi-ad-din Ardabili</a> (1252–1334). In 700/1301, Safi al-Din assumed the leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Zahediyeh" title="Zahediyeh">Zahediyeh</a>, a significant <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> order in Gilan, from his spiritual master and father-in-law <a href="/wiki/Zahed_Gilani" title="Zahed Gilani">Zahed Gilani</a>. Due to the great spiritual charisma of Safi al-Din, the order was later known as the Safaviyya. The Safavid order soon gained great influence in the city of Ardabil, and Hamdullah Mustaufi noted that most of the people of Ardabil were followers of Safi al-Din.</p>
<p>Religious poetry from Safi al-Din, written in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Azari_language" title="Old Azari language">Old Azari language</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yar_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yar-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>—a now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Northwestern Iranian language</a>—and accompanied by a paraphrase in Persian that helps its understanding, has survived to this day and has linguistic importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yar_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yar-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Safī al-Dīn, the leadership of the Safaviyya passed to <a href="/wiki/Sadr_al-D%C4%ABn_M%C5%ABs%C4%81" title="Sadr al-Dīn Mūsā">Sadr al-Dīn Mūsā</a> († 794/1391–92). The order at this time was transformed into a religious movement that conducted religious propaganda throughout Persia, Syria and Asia Minor, and most likely had maintained its Sunni Shafi’ite origin at that time. The leadership of the order passed from Sadr ud-Dīn Mūsā to his son Khwādja Ali († 1429) and in turn to his son Ibrāhīm († 1429–47).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qezelbash.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1609" data-file-width="923" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Qezelbash.JPG/170px-Qezelbash.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Qezelbash.JPG/255px-Qezelbash.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Qezelbash.JPG/340px-Qezelbash.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qezelbash.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mannequin of a Safavid <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> soldier, showing characteristic red cap (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%27dabad_Palace" title="Sa'dabad Palace">Sa'dabad Palace</a>, Teheran)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Shaykh_Junayd" title="Shaykh Junayd">Shaykh Junayd</a>, the son of Ibrāhim, assumed the leadership of the Safaviyya in 1447, the history of the Safavid movement was radically changed. According to R.M. Savory, "'Sheikh Junayd was not content with spiritual authority and he sought material power'". At that time, the most powerful dynasty in Persia was that of the <a href="/wiki/Kara_Koyunlu" title="Kara Koyunlu">Kara Koyunlu</a>, the "Black Sheep", whose ruler <a href="/wiki/Jahan_Shah" title="Jahan Shah">Jahan Shah</a> ordered Junāyd to leave <a href="/wiki/Ardabil" title="Ardabil">Ardabil</a> or else he would bring destruction and ruin upon the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> Junayd sought refuge with the rival of Kara Koyunlu Jahan Shah, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">Aq Qoyunlu (White Sheep Turkomans)</a> <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khan</a> <a href="/wiki/Uzun_Hassan" title="Uzun Hassan">Uzun Hassan</a>, and cemented his relationship by marrying Uzun Hassan's sister, Khadija Begum. Junayd was killed during an incursion into the territories of the <a href="/wiki/Shirvanshah" title="Shirvanshah">Shirvanshah</a> and was succeeded by his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haydar_Safavi" title="Haydar Safavi">Haydar Safavi</a>.</p>
<p>Haydar married Martha 'Alamshah Begom,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anthony_Bryer_1975_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anthony_Bryer_1975-32">[32]</a></sup> Uzun Hassan's daughter, who gave birth to <a href="/wiki/Ismail_I" title="Ismail I">Ismail I</a>, founder of the Safavid dynasty. Martha's mother Theodora—better known as <a href="/wiki/Despina_Khatun" title="Despina Khatun">Despina Khatun</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>—was a <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greek" title="Pontic Greek">Pontic Greek</a> princess, the daughter of the Grand <a href="/wiki/Komnenos" title="Komnenos">Komnenos</a> <a href="/wiki/John_IV_of_Trebizond" title="John IV of Trebizond">John IV of Trebizond</a>. She had been married to Uzun Hassan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> in exchange for protection of the Grand Komnenos from the Ottomans.</p>
<p>After Uzun Hassan's death, his son Ya'qub felt threatened by the growing Safavid religious influence. Ya'qub allied himself with the Shirvanshah and killed Haydar in 1488. By this time, the bulk of the Safaviyya were <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">nomadic Oghuz Turkic-speaking</a> clans from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan_(Iran)" title="Azerbaijan (Iran)">Azerbaijan</a> and were known as <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> "Red Heads" because of their distinct red headgear. The Qizilbash were warriors, spiritual followers of Haydar, and a source of the Safavid military and political power.</p>
<p>After the death of Haydar, the Safaviyya gathered around his son <a href="/wiki/Ali_Mirza_Safavi" title="Ali Mirza Safavi">Ali Mirza Safavi</a>, who was also pursued and subsequently killed by Ya'qub. According to official Safavid history, before passing away, Ali had designated his young brother Ismail as the spiritual leader of the Safaviyya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_of_the_dynasty_by_Sh.C4.81h_Ism.C4.81il_I_.28r._1501.E2.80.9324.29">Founding of the dynasty by Shāh Ismāil I (<i>r.</i> 1501–24)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ismail_I" title="Ismail I">Ismail I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz,_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1882" data-file-width="3292" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d5/Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg/250px-Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d5/Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg/375px-Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d5/Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg/500px-Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz%2C_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ismail_declares_himself_shah_by_entering_Tabriz,_Chingiz_Mehbaliyev.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ismail_I" title="Ismail I">Ismail</a> declares himself "Shah" by entering <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a>; his troops in front of <a href="/wiki/Arg_of_Tabriz" title="Arg of Tabriz">Arg of Tabriz</a>, painter <i>Chingiz Mehbaliyev</i>, in private collection.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia_prior_to_Ism.C4.81il.27s_rule">Persia prior to Ismāil's rule</span></h4>
<p>After the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a> (1370–1506), Persia was politically splintered, giving rise to a number of religious movements. The demise of Tamerlane's political authority created a space in which several religious communities, particularly Shi’i ones, could come to the fore and gain prominence. Among these were a number of Sufi brotherhoods, the <a href="/wiki/Hurufism" title="Hurufism">Hurufis</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nuqtawis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nuqtawis (page does not exist)">Nuqtawis</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Musha%E2%80%98sha%E2%80%98&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Musha‘sha‘ (page does not exist)">Musha‘sha‘</a>. Of these various movements, the Safavid Qizilbash was the most politically resilient, and due to its success Shah Isma’il I gained political prominence in 1501.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> There were many local states prior to the Iranian state established by Ismāil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The most important local rulers about 1500 were:</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Husayn_Bayqarah" title="Husayn Bayqarah">Huṣayn Bāyqarā</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> ruler of <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herāt</a></li>
<li>Alwand Mīrzā, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">Aq Qoyunlu</a> <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khan</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabrīz</a></li>
<li>Murad Beg, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aq_Qoyunlu" title="Aq Qoyunlu">Aq Qoyunlu</a> ruler of Irāq al-Ajam</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Farrukh_Yassar" title="Farrukh Yassar">Farro<u>kh</u> Yaṣar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Shirvanshah" title="Shirvanshah">Shah of Širvan</a></li>
<li>Badi Alzamān Mīrzā, local ruler of <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Bal<u>kh</u></a></li>
<li>Huṣayn Kīā Chalavī, the local ruler of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semnan_(city)" title="Semnan (city)">Semnān</a></li>
<li>Murād Beg Bayandar, local ruler of <a href="/wiki/Yazd" title="Yazd">Yazd</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sultan_Mahmud_ibn_Nizam_al-Din_Yahya" title="Sultan Mahmud ibn Nizam al-Din Yahya">Sultan Mahmud ibn Nizam al-Din Yahya</a>, ruler of <a href="/wiki/Sistan" title="Sistan">Sistan</a></li>
<li>Several local rulers of <a href="/wiki/Tabaristan" title="Tabaristan">Tabaristan</a> such as: Bisotun II, Ashraf ibn Taj al-Dawla, Mirza Ali, and Kia Husayn.</li>
</ul>
<p>Ismāil was able to unite all these lands under the Iranian Empire he created.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Sh.C4.81h_Ism.C4.81il_I">Rise of Shāh Ismāil I</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Ismail_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="993" data-file-width="776" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/220px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/330px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/440px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Ismail_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shah Ismail I</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="903" data-file-width="600" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg/220px-The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg/330px-The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg/440px-The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Battle_between_Shah_Ismail_and_Shaybani_Khan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ismail's battle with Uzbek warlord Muhammad Shaybani Khan in 1510, on a folio from the <i>Kebir Musaver Silsilname.</i> After the battle Ismail purportedly gilded the skull of Shaybani Khan for use as a wine goblet.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Safavid dynasty was founded about 1501 by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shah_Ismail_I" title="Shah Ismail I">Shāh Ismāil I.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismailsafaviiranica_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ismailsafaviiranica-55">[55]</a></sup> His background is disputed: the language he used is not identical with that of his "race" or "nationality" and he was bilingual from birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mino_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mino-56">[56]</a></sup> Ismāil was of mixed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azeri_people" title="Azeri people">Azeri</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greek</a> descent, although others argue that he had no Azeri ancestry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mino_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mino-56">[56]</a></sup> and was a direct descendant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> mystic <a href="/wiki/Safi-ad-din_Ardabili" title="Safi-ad-din Ardabili">Sheikh Safi al-Din</a>. As such, he was the last in the line of hereditary Grand Masters of the Safaviyeh order, prior to its ascent to a ruling dynasty. Ismāil was known as a brave and charismatic youth, zealous with regards to his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%E2%80%99a" title="Shi’a">Shi’a</a> faith, and believed himself to be of divine descent—practically worshipped by his <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> followers.</p>
<p>In 1500, Ismāil <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conquest_of_Shirvan" title="Safavid conquest of Shirvan">invaded</a> neighboring <a href="/wiki/Shirvan" title="Shirvan">Shirvan</a> to avenge the death of his father, Sheik Haydar, who had been murdered in 1488 by the ruling Shirvanshah, Farrukh Yassar. Afterwards, Ismail went on a conquest campaign, capturing <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a> in July 1501, where he enthroned himself the Shāh of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Azerbaijan" title="Iranian Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> proclaimed himself Shahanshah of Iran<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and minted coins in his name, proclaiming Shi’ism the official religion of his domain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> The establishment of Shi’ism as the state religion led to various Sufi orders openly declaring their Shi’i position, and others to promptly assume Shi’ism. Among these, the founder of one of the most successful Sufi orders, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Nimatullah_Wali" title="Shah Nimatullah Wali">Ni’matullah</a> (d. 1431), traced his descent from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismaili" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammad_b._Ismail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Muhammad b. Ismail (page does not exist)">Muhammad b. Ismail</a>, as evidenced in a poem as well as another unpublished literary composition. Though Nimatullah was apparently Sunni, the Ni’matullahi order soon declared his order to be Shi’i after the rise of the Safavid dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Ismail I initially gained mastery over Azerbaijan alone, the Safavids ultimately won the struggle for power over all of Persia, which had been going on for nearly a century between various dynasties and political forces. A year after his victory in Tabriz, Ismāil claimed most of Persia as part of his territory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> and within 10 years established a complete control over all of it. Ismail followed the line of Iranian and Turkmen rulers prior to his assumption of the title "Padishah-i-Iran", previously held by Uzun Hasan and many other Iranian kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The Ottoman sultans addressed him as <i>the king of Persian lands and the heir to <a href="/wiki/Jamshid" title="Jamshid">Jamshid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kai_Khosrow" title="Kai Khosrow">Kai Khosrow</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Having started with just the possession of Azerbaijan, <a href="/wiki/Shirvan" title="Shirvan">Shirvan</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a> (with its important city of <a href="/wiki/Derbent" title="Derbent">Derbent</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> in 1501,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erzincan" title="Erzincan">Erzincan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erzurum" title="Erzurum">Erzurum</a> fell into his power in 1502,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a> in 1503, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiraz,_Iran" title="Shiraz, Iran">Shiraz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerman" title="Kerman">Kerman</a> in 1504, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diyarbakir" title="Diyarbakir">Diyarbakir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a> in 1507, <a href="/wiki/Van_Province" title="Van Province">Van</a> in 1508, <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in 1509, and <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a>, as well as other parts of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>, in 1510. In 1503, the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kartli" title="Kingdom of Kartli">Kartli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kakheti" title="Kingdom of Kakheti">Kakheti</a> were made his vassals as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> By 1511, the <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> in the north-east, led by their Khan <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shaybani" title="Muhammad Shaybani">Muhammad Shaybāni</a>, were driven far to the north, across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus_River" title="Oxus River">Oxus River</a>, where they continued to attack the Safavids. Ismail's decisive victory over the Uzbeks, who had occupied most of Khorasan, ensured Iran's eastern borders, and the Uzbeks never since expanded beyond the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindukush" title="Hindukush">Hindukush</a>. Although the Uzbeks continued to make occasional raids to Khorasan, the Safavid empire was able to keep them at bay throughout its reign.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_clashes_with_the_Ottomans">Start of clashes with the Ottomans</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaldiran" title="Battle of Chaldiran">Battle of Chaldiran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chaldiran_(1514).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="800" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Chaldiran_%281514%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chaldiran_(1514).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artwork of the Battle of Chaldiran.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>More problematic for the Safavids was the powerful neighboring <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The Ottomans, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> dynasty, considered the active recruitment of Turkmen tribes of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> for the Safavid cause as a major threat. To counter the rising Safavid power, in 1502, <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Sultan Bayezid II</a> forcefully deported many Shi'as from Anatolia to other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> realm. In 1511, there was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9Eahkulu_Rebellion" title="Şahkulu Rebellion">widespread pro-Shia and pro-Safavid uprising</a> directed against the Ottoman Empire from within the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Furthermore, by the early 1510s Ismail's expansionistic policies had pushed the Safavid borders in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> even more westwards. The Ottomans soon reacted with a large-scale incursion into Eastern Anatolia by Safavid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghazis" title="Ghazis">ghazis</a> under Nūr-ʿAlī Ḵalīfa. This action coincided with the accession to the Ottoman throne in 1512 of Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayezid_II" title="Bayezid II">Bayezid</a>'s son, and it was the <a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a> leading to Selim's decision to invade neighbouring Safavid Iran two years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In 1514, Sultan Selim I marched through Anatolia and reached the plain of Chaldiran near the city of <a href="/wiki/Khoy" title="Khoy">Khoy</a>, where a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaldiran" title="Battle of Chaldiran">decisive battle</a> was fought. Most sources agree that the Ottoman army was at least double the size of that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shah_Ismail_I" title="Shah Ismail I">Ismāil</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismailsafaviiranica_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-ismailsafaviiranica-55">[55]</a></sup> however, the Ottomans had the advantage of artillery, which the Safavid army lacked. According to R. M. Savory, <i>"Salim's plan was to winter at Tabriz and complete the conquest of Persia the following spring. However, a mutiny among his officers who refused to spend the winter at Tabriz forced him to withdraw across territory laid waste by the Safavid forces, eight days later"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismailsafaviiranica_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-ismailsafaviiranica-55">[55]</a></sup> Although Ismāil was defeated and his capital was captured, the Safavid empire survived. The war between the two powers continued under Ismāil's son, <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_I" title="Tahmasp I">Shāh Tahmāsp I</a>, and the Ottoman Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suleiman_I" title="Suleiman I">Suleiman I</a>, until Shāh Abbās retook the area lost to the Ottomans by 1602.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Safavid_persia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="1033" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Map_Safavid_persia.png/220px-Map_Safavid_persia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Map_Safavid_persia.png/330px-Map_Safavid_persia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Map_Safavid_persia.png/440px-Map_Safavid_persia.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Safavid_persia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shāh Ismāil's empire</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The consequences of the defeat at Chaldiran were also psychological for Ismāil: the defeat destroyed Ismāil's belief in his invincibility, based on his claimed divine status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-savoryeiref_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-savoryeiref-26">[26]</a></sup> His relationships with his Qizilbash followers were also fundamentally altered. The tribal rivalries between the Qizilbash, which temporarily ceased before the defeat at Chaldiran, resurfaced in intense form immediately after the death of Ismāil, and led to ten years of civil war (930-40/1524-33) until Shāh Tahmāsp regained control of the affairs of the state. For most of the last decade of Ismail's reign, the domestic affairs of the empire were overseen by the Tajik vizier <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Shah_Husayn" title="Mirza Shah Husayn">Mirza Shah Husayn Isfahani</a> until his assassination in 1523.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streu_146_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Streu_146-71">[71]</a></sup> The Chaldiran battle also holds historical significance as the start of over 300 years of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman-Persian_Wars" title="Ottoman-Persian Wars">frequent and harsh warfare</a> fuelled by geo-politics and ideological differences between the Ottomans and the Iranian Safavids (as well as successive Iranian states) mainly regarding territories in Eastern Anatolia, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.</p>
<p>Early Safavid power in Iran was based on the military power of the Qizilbash. Ismāil exploited the first element to seize power in Iran. But eschewing politics after his defeat in Chaldiran, he left the affairs of the government to the office of the <i>wakīl</i> (chief administrator, <i>vakīl</i> in Turkish). Ismāil's successors, most manifestly Shāh Abbās I, successfully diminished the influence of the Qizilbash on the affairs of the state.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sh.C4.81h_Tahm.C4.81sp_.28r._1524.E2.80.9376.29">Shāh Tahmāsp (<i>r.</i> 1524–76)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shah_Tahmasp" title="Shah Tahmasp">Shah Tahmasp</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_Strife_during_Tahm.C4.81sp.27s_Early_Reign">Civil Strife during Tahmāsp's Early Reign</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tahmasb-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="443" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tahmasb-1.jpg/220px-Tahmasb-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tahmasb-1.jpg/330px-Tahmasb-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Tahmasb-1.jpg/440px-Tahmasb-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tahmasb-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shah Tahmasp, fresco on the walls of the <a href="/wiki/Chehel_Sotoun" title="Chehel Sotoun">Chehel Sotoun</a> Palace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shāh Tahmāsp, the young titular governor of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> succeeded his father Ismāil in 1524, when he was ten years and three months old. The succession was evidently undisputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streu_146_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Streu_146-71">[71]</a></sup> Tahmāsp was the <a href="/wiki/Ward_(law)" title="Ward (law)">ward</a> of the powerful Qizilbash <i>amir</i> Ali Beg Rūmlū (titled <i>"<a href="/wiki/Div_Sultan_Rumlu" title="Div Sultan Rumlu">Div Soltān Rumlu</a>"</i>) who saw himself as the <i>de facto</i> ruler of the state. Rūmlū and Kopek Sultān Ustajlu (who had been Ismail's last <i>wakīl</i>) established themselves as co-regents of the young shah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streu_146_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Streu_146-71">[71]</a></sup> The Qizilbash, which still suffered under the legacy of the battle of Chaldiran, was engulfed in internal rivalries. The first two years of Tahmāsp's reign was consumed with Div Sultān’s efforts to eliminate Ustajlu from power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streu_146_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-Streu_146-71">[71]</a></sup> This court intrigue lead directly to tribal conflict. Beginning in 1526 periodic battles broke out, beginning in northwest Persia but soon involving all of Khorasan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In the absence of a charismatic, messianic rallying figure like the young Ismail, the tribal leaders reclaimed their traditional prerogative and threatened to return to the time of local warlords. For nearly 10 years rival Qizilbash factions fought each other. Af first, Kopek Sultān's Ustajlu tribe suffered the heaviest, and he himself was killed in a battle.</p>
<p>Thus Div Soltān emerged victorious in the first palace struggle, bit he fell victim to Chuha Sultān of the Takkalu, who turned Tahmāsp against his first mentor. In 1527 Tahmāsp demonstrated his desire by shooting an arrow at Div Soltān before the assembled court. The Takkalu replaced the Rumlu as the dominant tribe. They in turn would be replaced by the Shamlu, whose amir, Husain Khan, became the chief adviser. This latest leader would only last until 1534, when he was deposed and executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the downfall of Husain Khan, Tahmāsp asserted his rule. Rather than rely on another Turkmen tribe, he appointed a Persian <i>wakīl</i>. From 1553 for forty years the shah was able to avoid being ensnared in tribal treacheries. But the decade of civil war had exposed the empire to foreign danger and Tahmāsp had to turn his attention to the repeated raids by the Uzbeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_Threats_to_the_Empire">Foreign Threats to the Empire</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman-Safavid_War_(1532-1555)" title="Ottoman-Safavid War (1532-1555)">Ottoman-Safavid War (1532-1555)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Peace of Amasya</a></div>
<p>The Uzbeks, during the reign of Tahmāsp, attacked the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">eastern provinces of the kingdom</a> five times, and the Ottomans under <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Soleymān I</a> invaded Persia four times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Decentralized control over Uzbek forces was largely responsible for the inability of the Uzbeks to make territorial inroads into Khorasan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Putting aside internal dissension, the Safavid nobles responded to a threat to Herat in 1528 by riding eastward with Tahmāsp (then 17) and soundly defeating the numerically superior forces of the Uzbeks at Jām.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The victory resulted at least in part from Safavid use of firearms, which they had been acquiring and drilling with since Chaldiran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Notwithstanding the success with firearms at Jām, Tahmāsp still lacked the confidence to engage their archrivals the Ottomans, choosing instead to cede territory, often using <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> tactics in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The goal of the Ottomans in the 1534 and 1548-1549 campaigns, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman-Safavid_War_(1532-1555)" title="Ottoman-Safavid War (1532-1555)">1532-1555 Ottoman-Safavid War</a>, was to install Tahmāsp's brothers (Sam Mirza and <a href="/wiki/Alqas_Mirza" title="Alqas Mirza">Alqas Mirza</a>, respectively) as shah in order to make Persia a vassal state. Although in those campaigns (and in 1554) the Ottomans captured <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a>, they lacked a communications line sufficient to occupy it for long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Nevertheless, given the insecurity in Iraq and its northwest territory, Tahmāsp moved his court from Tabriz to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qazvin_(city)" title="Qazvin (city)">Qazvin</a>.</p>
<p>In the gravest crisis of Tahmāsp's reign, Ottoman forces in 1553-54 captured <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">Yerevan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karabakh" title="Karabakh">Karabakh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nakhchivan_Autonomous_Republic#Iranian_rule" title="Nakhchivan Autonomous Republic">Nakhjuwan</a>, destroyed palaces, villas and gardens, and threatened <a href="/wiki/Ardabil" title="Ardabil">Ardabil</a>. During these operations an agent of the Samlu (now supporting Sam Mizra's pretentions) attempted to poison the shah. Tahmāsp resolved to end hostilities and sent his ambassador to Soleymān's winter quarters in <a href="/wiki/Erzurum" title="Erzurum">Erzurum</a> in September 1554 to sue for peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Temporary terms were followed by the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Peace of Amasya</a> in June 1555, ending the war with the Ottomans for the next two decades. The treaty was the first formal diplomatic recognition of the Safavid Empire by the Ottomans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Under the Peace, the Ottomans agreed to restore Yerevan, Karabakh and Nakhjuwan to the Safavids and in turn would retain Mesopotamia (Iraq) and eastern Anatolia. Soleymān agreed to permit Safavid Shi’a pilgrims to make pilgrimages to Mecca and Medina as well as tombs of imams in Iraq and Arabia on condition that the shah would abolish the <i>taburru,</i> the cursing of the first three Rashidun caliphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> It was a heavy price in terms of territory and prestige lost, but it allowed the empire to last, something that seemed improbable during the first years of Tahmāsp's reign.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_refugees:_Bayezid_and_Humayun">Royal refugees: Bayezid and Humayun</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1666" data-file-width="1000" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg/220px-Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg/330px-Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg/440px-Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Tahmasp_and_Humayun.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shah Tahmasp greets the exiled Humayun</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Almost simultaneously with the emergence of the Safavid Empire, the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>, founded by the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> heir <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a>, was developing in South-Asia. The Mughals adhered (for the most part) to a tolerant Sunni Islam while ruling a largely <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> population. After the death of Babur, his son <a href="/wiki/Humayun" title="Humayun">Humayun</a> was ousted from his territories and threatened by his half-brother and rival, who had inherited the northern part of Babur's territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards_11_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_11-85">[85]</a></sup> Having to flee from city to city, Humayun eventually sought refuge at the court of Tahmāsp in Qazvin in 1543. Tahmāsp received Humayun as the true emperor of the Mughal dynasty, despite the fact that Humayun had been living in exile for more than fifteen years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards_11_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_11-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> After Humayun converted to Shia Islam (under extreme duress),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards_11_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_11-85">[85]</a></sup> Tahmāsp offered him military assistance to regain his territories in return for <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>, which controlled the overland trade route between central Persia and the Ganges. In 1545 a combined Persian-Mughal force managed to seize Kandahar and occupy Kabul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Humayun handed over Kandahar, but Tahmāsp was forced to retake it in 1558, after Humayun seized it on the death of the Safavid governor.</p>
<p>Humayun was not the only royal figure to seek refuge at Tahmasp's court. A dispute arose in the Ottoman Empire over who was to succeed the aged <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a>. Suleiman's favourite wife, <a href="/wiki/Roxelana" title="Roxelana">Hürrem Sultan</a>, was eager for her son, <a href="/wiki/Selim_II" title="Selim II">Selim</a>, to become the next sultan. But Selim was an alcoholic and Hürrem's other son, <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eehzade_Bayezid" title="Şehzade Bayezid">Bayezid</a>, had shown far greater military ability. The two princes quarrelled and eventually Bayezid rebelled against his father. His letter of remorse never reached Suleiman, and he was forced to flee abroad to avoid execution. In 1559 Bayezid arrived in Iran where Tahmasp gave him a warm welcome. Suleiman was eager to negotiate his son's return, but Tahmasp rejected his promises and threats until, in 1561, Suleiman compromised with him. In September of that year, Tahmasp and Bayezid were enjoying a banquet at Tabriz when Tahmasp suddenly pretended he had received news that the Ottoman prince was engaged in a plot against his life. An angry mob gathered and Tahmasp had Bayezid put into custody, alleging it was for his own safety. Tahmasp then handed the prince over to the Ottoman ambassador. Shortly afterwards, Bayezid was killed by agents sent by his own father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_of_Shah_Tahmasp">Legacy of Shah Tahmasp</span></h4>
<p>When the young Shah Tahmāsp took the throne, Persia was in a dire state. But in spite of a weak economy, a civil war and foreign wars on two fronts, Tahmāsp managed to retain his crown and maintain the territorial integrity of the empire (although much reduced from Ismail's time). During the first 30 years of his long reign, he was able to suppress the internal divisions by exerting control over a strengthened central military force. In the war against the Uzbeks he showed that the Safavids had become a <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder_Empires" title="Gunpowder Empires">gunpowder empire</a>. His tactics in dealing with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> threat eventually allowed for a treaty which preserved peace for twenty years.</p>
<p>In cultural matters, Tahmāsp presided the revival of the fine arts, which flourished under his patronage. Safavid culture is often admired for the large-scale city planning and architecture, achievements made during the reign of later shahs, but the arts of <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">persian miniature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book-binding" title="Book-binding">book-binding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, in fact, never received as much attention as they did during his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tahmāsp also planted the seeds that would, unintentionally, produce change much later. During his reign he had realized while both looking to his own empire and that of the neighboring Ottomans, that there were dangerous rivalling factions and internal family rivalries that were a threat to the heads of state. Not taken care of accordingly, these were a serious threat to the ruler, or worse, could bring the fall of the former or could lead to unnecessary court intrigues. According to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Iranica" title="Encyclopedia Iranica">Encyclopedia Iranica</a></i>, for Tahmāsp, the problem circled around the military tribal elite of the empire, the <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qezelbāš</a>, who believed that physical proximity to and control of a member of the immediate Safavid family guaranteed spiritual advantages, political fortune, and material advancement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup> Despite that Tahmāsp could nullify and neglect some of his consternations regarding potential issues related to his family by having his close direct male relatives such as his brothers and sons routinely transferred around to various governorships in the empire, he understood and realized that any long-term solutions would mainly involve minimizing the political and military presence of the Qezelbāš as a whole. According to <i>Encyclopedia Iranica</i>, his father and founder of the Empire, Ismail I, had begun this process on a bureaucratic level as he appointed a number of prominent Persians in powerful bureaucratic positions, and one can see this continued in Tahmāsp’s lengthy and close relationship with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Safavid_Viziers" title="List of Safavid Viziers">chief vizier</a>, Qāżi Jahān of Qazvin, after 1535.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup> While Persians continued to fill their historical role as administrators and clerical elites under Tahmāsp, little had been done so far to minimize the military role of the Qezelbāš.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup> Therefore, in 1540, Shah Tahmāsp started the first of a series of invasions of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> region, both meant as a training and drilling for his soldiers, as well as mainly bringing back massive numbers of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> slaves, who would form the basis of a military slave system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Streusand.2C_p._148_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Streusand.2C_p._148-91">[91]</a></sup> alike to the <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">janissaries</a> of the neighbouring Ottoman Empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-92">[92]</a></sup> as well as at the same time forming a new layer in Iranian society composed of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Peoples_of_the_Caucasus" title="Peoples of the Caucasus">Caucasians</a>. At the fourth invasion in 1553, it was now clear that Tahmāsp followed a policy of annexation and resettlement as he gained control over <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a> (Tiflis) and the region of <a href="/wiki/Kartli" title="Kartli">Kartli</a> while physically transplanting more than 30,000 people to the central Iranian heartlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup> According to <i>Encyclopedia Iranica</i>, this would be the starting point for the corps of the <i>ḡolāmān-e ḵāṣṣa-ye-e šarifa</i>, or <i>royal slaves</i>, who would dominate the Safavid military for most of the empire's length. As non-Turcoman converts to Islam, these Circassian and Georgian <a href="/wiki/Ghilman" title="Ghilman">ḡolāmāns</a> (also written as <i>ghulams</i>) were completely unrestrained by clan loyalties and kinship obligations, which was an attractive feature for a ruler like Tahmāsp whose childhood and upbringing had been deeply affected by Qezelbāš tribal politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup> In turn, many of these transplanted women became wives and concubines of Tahmāsp, and the Safavid harem emerged as a competitive, and sometimes lethal, arena of ethnic politics as cliques of Turkmen, Circassian, and Georgian women and courtiers vied with each other for the shah’s attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahm.C4.81sp_I_90-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tahm.C4.81sp_I-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the first slave soldiers would not be organized until the reign of Abbas I, during Tahmāsp's time Caucasians would already become important members of the royal household, <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">Harem</a> and in the civil and military administration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manz_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manz-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-94">[94]</a></sup> and by that becoming their way of eventually becoming an integral part of the society. One of Tahmāsp's sisters married a Circassian, who would use his court office to team up with Tahmāsp's daughter, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pari_Kh%C4%81n_Kh%C4%81num" title="Pari Khān Khānum">Pari Khān Khānum</a> to assert themselves in succession matters after Tahmāsp's death.</p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Peace of Amasya</a>, Tasmāsp underwent what he called a "sincere repentance." Tasmāsp at the same time removed his son Ismail from his Qizilbash followers and imprisoned him at Qahqaha. Moreover, he began to strengthen Shia practice by such things as forbidding in the new capital of Qazvin poetry and music which did not esteem Ali and the Twelve Imams. He also reduced the taxes of districts that were traditionally Shia, regulated services in mosques and engaged Shia propagandists and spies. Extortion, intimidation and harassment were practiced against Sunnis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Tahmāsp died in 984/1576, Persia was calm domestically, with secure borders and no imminent threat from either the Uzbeks or the Ottomans. What remained unchanged, however, was the constant threat of local disaffection with the weak central authority. That condition would not change (and in fact it would worsen) until Tahmāsp's grandson, Abbas I, assumed the throne.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chaos_under_Tahmasp.E2.80.99s_sons">Chaos under Tahmasp’s sons</span></h3>
<p>On Tahmāsp’s death support for a successor coalesced around two of his nine sons; the support divided on ethnic lines—<a href="/wiki/Ismail_II" title="Ismail II">Ismail</a> was supported by most of the Turkmen tribes as well as his sister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pari_Kh%C4%81n_Kh%C4%81num" title="Pari Khān Khānum">Pari Khān Khānum</a>, her Circassian uncle <a href="/wiki/Shamkhal_Sultan" title="Shamkhal Sultan">Shamkhal Sultan</a> as well as the rest of the Circassians, while Haydar was mostly supported by the Georgians at court although he also had support from the Turkmen Ustajlu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Ismail had been imprisoned at Qahqaha since 1556 by his father on charges of plotting a coup, but his selection was ensured when 30,000 Qizilbash supporters demonstrated outside the prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Shortly after the installation of Ismail II on August 22, 1576, Haydar was beheaded.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ismail_II_.28r._1576.E2.80.9377.29">Ismail II (<i>r.</i> 1576–77)</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ismail_II" title="Ismail II">Ismail II</a></div>
<p>Ismail’s 14-month reign was notable for two things: continual bloodletting of his relatives and others (including his own supporters) and his reversal on religion. He had all his relatives killed except for his older brother, Muhammad Khudabanda, who, being nearly blind, was not a real candidate for the throne, and Muhammad’s three sons, Hamza Mirza, Abbas Mirza and Abu Talib Mirza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> While the murderous actions of Ismail might be explained by political prudence (Ottoman sultans occasionally purged the bloodline to prevent succession rivals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>), his actions against Shi’a suggest retaliation against his father, who saw himself as a pious practitioner. Ismail sought to reintroduce Sunni orthodoxy. But even here there may have been practical political considerations; namely, “concern about the excessively powerful position of Shi‘i dignitaries, which would have been undermined by a reintroduction of the Sunna.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> His conduct might also be explained by his drug use. In any event, he was ultimately killed (according to some accounts) by his Circassian half-sister, <a href="/wiki/Pari_Khan_Khanum" title="Pari Khan Khanum">Pari Khān Khānum</a>, who championed him over Haydar. She is said to have poisoned his opium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mohammad_Khodabanda_.28r._1578.E2.80.9387.29">Mohammad Khodabanda (<i>r.</i> 1578–87)</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Khodabanda" title="Mohammad Khodabanda">Mohammad Khodabanda</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jealousy_among_Rivals.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2327" data-file-width="1548" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Jealousy_among_Rivals.png/220px-Jealousy_among_Rivals.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Jealousy_among_Rivals.png/330px-Jealousy_among_Rivals.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Jealousy_among_Rivals.png/440px-Jealousy_among_Rivals.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jealousy_among_Rivals.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Jealousy among Rivals" attributed to Muhammadi. Miniature painting contained in a Persian volume entitled <i>Busta</i> by Sa'di in 1579, possibly under the patronage of Vizier Mirza Salman. E.M. Soudavar Trust, Houston, Texas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the death of Ismail II there were three candidates for succession: Shāh Shujā', the infant son of Ismail (only a few weeks old), Ismail's brother, Muhammad Khudabanda; and Muhammad’s son, Sultan Hamza Mirza, 11 years old at the time. Pari Khān Khānum, sister of Ismail and Muhammad, hoped to act as regent for any of the three (including her older brother, who was nearly blind). Muhammad was selected and received the crown on February 11, 1579.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Muhammad would rule for 10 years, and his sister at first dominated the court, but she fell in the first of many intrigues which continued even though the Uzbeks and Ottomans again used the opportunity to threaten Safavid territory.</p>
<p>Muhammad allowed others to direct the affairs of state, but none of them had either the prestige, skill or ruthlessness of either Tahmāsp or Ismail II to rein in the ethnic or palace factions, and each of his rulers met grim ends. Muhammad's younger sister, who had a hand in elevating and deposing Ismail II and thus had considerable influence among the Qizilbash, was the first. She did not last much longer than Muhammad's installation at Qazvin, where she was murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roem_255_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roem_255-103">[103]</a></sup> She was done in by intrigues by the vizier Mirza Salman (who was a holdover from Ismail II's reign) and Muhammad's chief wife <a href="/wiki/Khayr_al-Nisa_Begum" title="Khayr al-Nisa Begum">Khayr al-Nisa Begum</a>, known as Mahd-i ‘Ulyā. There is some indication that Mirza Salman was the chief conspirator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pari_Khan_Khanum" title="Pari Khan Khanum">Pari Khān Khānum</a> could master strong support among the Qizilbash, and her uncle, <a href="/wiki/Shamkhal_Sultan" title="Shamkhal Sultan">Shamkhal Sultan</a>, was a prominent <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a> who held a high official position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Mirza Salman left the capital before Pari Khān Khānum closed the gates and was able to meet Muhammad Khudabanda and his wife in Shiraz, to whom he offered his services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> He may have believed that he would rule once their enemy was disposed of, but Mahd-i ‘Ulyā proved the stronger of the two.</p>
<blockquote>
<p>She was by no means content to exercise a more or less indirect influence on affairs of state: instead, she openly carried out all essential functions herself, including the appointment of the chief officers of the realm. In place of the usual royal audience, these high dignitaries had to assemble each morning at the entrance to the women’s apartments in order to receive the Begum’s orders. On these occasions the royal edicts were drawn up and sealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roem_255_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roem_255-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The amirs demanded that she will be removed, and Mahd-i Ulya was strangled in the harem in July 1579 on the ground of an alleged affair with the brother of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_khanate" title="Crimean khanate">Crimean khan</a>, Adil Giray,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roem_255_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roem_255-103">[103]</a></sup> who was captured during the 1578-1590 Ottoman war and held captive in the capital, Qazvin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESicker20012-3_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESicker20012-3-107">[107]</a></sup> None of the perpetrators were brought to justice, although the shah lectured the assembled amirs on how they departed from the old ways when the shah was master to his Sufi disciples. The shah used that occasion to proclaim the 11-year-old Sultan Hamza Mirza (Mahd-i ‘Ulyā's favorite) crown-prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The palace intrigues reflected ethnic unrest which would soon erupt into open warfare. Persia's neighbors improved upon the opportunity to attack Persia. The Uzbeks struck in the Spring of 1578 but were repelled by Murtaza Quli Sultan, governor of Mashhad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> More seriously the Ottomans ended the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Amasya" title="Peace of Amasya">Peace of Amasya</a> and commenced <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1578%E2%80%9390)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1578–90)">a war with Persia that would last until 1590</a> by invading Iran's territories of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shirvan" title="Shirvan">Shirvan</a>. While the initial attacks were repelled, the Ottomans continued and grabbed considerable territory in <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, Kurdistan and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luristan" title="Luristan">Luristan</a> and in 993/1585 they even took <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the midst of these foreign perils, rebellion broke out in Khorasan fomented by (or on behalf of) Muhammad's son, Abbas. Ali Quli Khan Shamlu, the <a href="/wiki/Lala_(title)" title="Lala (title)"><i>lala</i></a> of Abbas and Ismail II's man in Herat proclaimed Abbas shah there April 1581.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The following year the loyal Qizilbash forces (the Turkmen and Takkalu who controlled Qazvin), with vizier Mirza Salam and crown prince Sultan Hamza Mirza at their head to confront the rebelling Ustajlu-Shamlu coalition which had assumed control of Khorasan under the nominal rule of young Abbas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The Ustajlu chief, Murshid Quli Khan, immediately acquiesced and received a royal pardon. Shumlu leader, Ali Quli Khan, however, holed himself inside Herat with Abbas. The vizier thought that the royal forces failed to prosecute the siege sufficiently and accused the forces of sedition. The loyal Qizibash recoiled at their treatment by Mirza Salam, who they resented for a number of reasons (not least of which was the fact that a Tajik was given military command over them), and demanded that he be turned over to them. The crown prince (the vizier's son-in-law) meekly turned him over, and the Qizilbash executed him and confiscated his property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The siege of Herat thus ended in 1583 without Ali Quli Khan backing down and Khorasan was in a state of open rebellion.</p>
<p>In 1585 two events occurred that would combine to break the impasse among the Qizilbash. First, in the west, the Ottomans, seeing the disarray of the warriors, pressed deep into Safavid territory and occupied the old capital of Tabriz. Crown prince Hamza Mirza, now 21 years and director of Safavid affairs, led a force to confront the Ottomans, but in 1586 was murdered under mysterious circumstances. In the east Murshid Quli Khan, of the Ustajlu tribe, managed to snatch Abbas away from the Shamlus. Two years later in 1587, the massive invasion of Khorasan by the Uzbeks proved the occasion whereby Murshid Quli Khan would make a play for supremacy in <a href="/wiki/Qazvin" title="Qazvin">Qazvin</a>. When he reached the capital with Abbas a public demonstration in the boy's favor decided the issue, and Shah Muhammad voluntarily handed over the insignia of kingship to his son, who was crowned Abbas I on October 1, 1588. The moment was grave for the empire, with the Ottomans deep in Persian territory in the west and north and the Uzbeks in possession of half of Khorasan in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shah_Abbas_.28r._1588.E2.80.931629.29">Shah Abbas (<i>r.</i> 1588–1629)</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia" title="Abbas I of Persia">Abbas I of Persia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1164" data-file-width="736" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg/170px-Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg/255px-Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg/340px-Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Abbas_I_engraving_by_Dominicus_Custos_-_Antwerp_artist_printer_and_engraver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Shah ‘Abbās King of the Persians</i>.<br/>
Copper engraving by <a href="/wiki/Dominicus_Custos" title="Dominicus Custos">Dominicus Custos</a>, <i>Atrium heroicum Caesarum</i> (1600–2).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 16-year-old Abbas I was installed as nominal shah in 1588, but the real power was intended to remain in the hands of his "mentor," Murshid Quli Kan, who reorganized court offices and principal governorships among the Qizilbash<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> and took the title of <i>wakīl</i> for himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Abbas' own position seemed even more dependent on Qizilbash approval than even Muhammad Khodabanda's was. The dependence of Abbas on the Qizilbash (which provided the only military force) was further reinforced by the precarious situation of the empire, in the vice of Ottoman and Uzbek territorial plunder. Yet over the course of ten years Abbas was able, using cautiously-timed but nonetheless decisive steps, to affect a profound transformation of Safavid administration and military, throw back the foreign invaders, and preside over a flourishing of Persian art.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_of_central_authority">Restoration of central authority</span></h4>
<p>Whether Abbas had fully formed his strategy at the onset, at least in retrospect his method of restoring the shah's authority involved three phases: (1) restoration of internal security and law and order; (2) recovery of the eastern territories from the Uzbek's; and (3) recovery of the western territories from the Ottomans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Before he could begin to embark on the first stage, he needed relief from the most serious threat to the empire—the military pressure from the Ottomans. He did so by taking the humiliating step of coming to peace terms with the Ottomans by making, for now, permanent their territorial gains in Iraq and the territories in the north, including Azerbaijan, <a href="/wiki/Qarabagh_District,_Ghazni" title="Qarabagh District, Ghazni">Qarabagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganja,_Azerbaijan" title="Ganja, Azerbaijan">Ganja</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> (comprising the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kartli" title="Kingdom of Kartli">Kingdom of Kartli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kakheti" title="Kingdom of Kakheti">Kakheti</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, and Kurdistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> At the same time, he took steps to ensure that the <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> did not mistake this apparent show of weakness as a signal for more tribal rivalry at the court. Although no one could have bristled more at the power grab of his "mentor" Murshid Quli Khan, he rounded up the leaders of a plot to assassinate the <i>wakīl</i> and had them executed. Then, having made the point that he would not encourage rivalries even purporting to favor his interests, he felt secure enough to have Murshid Quli Khan assassinated on his own orders in July 1589.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> It was clear that the style of leadership would be entirely different than Muhammad Khodabanda's leadership.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matthaus_1598.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="922" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Matthaus_1598.jpg/200px-Matthaus_1598.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Matthaus_1598.jpg/300px-Matthaus_1598.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Matthaus_1598.jpg/400px-Matthaus_1598.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allahverdi_khan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="555" data-file-width="555" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Allahverdi_khan.jpg/200px-Allahverdi_khan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Allahverdi_khan.jpg/300px-Allahverdi_khan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Allahverdi_khan.jpg/400px-Allahverdi_khan.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allahverdi_khan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
