Statue of <a href="/wiki/Allahverdi_Khan" title="Allahverdi Khan">Allahverdi Khan</a> (surnamed <a href="/wiki/Undiladze" title="Undiladze">Undiladze</a>) in Isfahan. Being one if not the most notable <i>ghulam</i> of the Safavid era, he functioned as statesman and the commander of the armed forces from 1598 until his death in 1613.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abbas was able to begin gradually transforming the empire from a tribal confederation to a modern imperial government by transferring provinces from <i>mamalik</i> (provincial) rule governed by a Qizilbash chief and the revenue of which mostly supported local Qizilbash administration and forces to <i>khass</i> (central) rule presided over by a court appointee and the revenue of which reverted to the court. Particularly important in this regard were the <a href="/wiki/Gilan_Province" title="Gilan Province">Gilan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazandaran_province" title="Mazandaran province">Mazandaran</a> provinces, which produced Persia's single most important export; silk. With the substantial new revenue, Abbas was able to build up a central, standing army, loyal only to him. This freed him of his dependence on Qizilbash warriors loyal to local tribal chiefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hondius_1610.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="649" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hondius_1610.jpg/200px-Hondius_1610.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hondius_1610.jpg/300px-Hondius_1610.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Hondius_1610.jpg/400px-Hondius_1610.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hondius_1610.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Safavid Persia, in 1610</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>What effectively fully severed Abbas's dependence on the Qizilbash, however, was how he constituted this new army. In order not to favor one Turkic tribe over another and to avoid inflaming the Turk-Persian enmity, he recruited his army from the "third force", a policy that had been implemented in its <i>baby-steps</i> since the reign of Tahmasp I—the <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> and to a lesser extent Armenian <i><a href="/wiki/Ghilman" title="Ghilman">ghulāms</a></i> (slaves) which (after conversion to Islam) were trained for the military or some branch of the civil or military administration. The standing army created by Abbas consisted of: (1) 10,000-15,000 cavalry <i>ghulām</i> regiments solely composed of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Peoples_of_the_Caucasus" title="Peoples of the Caucasus">Caucasians</a>, armed with muskets in addition to the usual weapons (then the largest cavalry in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>); (2) a corps of musketeers, <i>tufangchiyān</i>, mainly Iranians, originally foot soldiers but eventually mounted, and (3) a corps of artillerymen, <i>tūpchiyān</i>. Both corps of musketeers and artillerymen totaled 12,000 men. In addition the shah's personal bodyguard, made up exclusively of Caucasian <i>ghulāms</i>, was dramatically increased to 3,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> This force of well-trained Caucasian ghulams under Abbas amounted to a total of near 40,000 soldiers paid for and beholden to the Shah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savory_1980_79_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savory_1980_79-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BN142_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-BN142-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abbas also greatly increased the number of cannons at his disposal, permitting him to field 500 in a single battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BN142_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-BN142-125">[125]</a></sup> Ruthless discipline was enforced and looting was severely punished. Abbas was also able to draw on military advice from a number of European envoys, particularly from the English adventurers Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Shirley" title="Anthony Shirley">Anthony Shirley</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shirley" title="Robert Shirley">Robert Shirley</a>, who arrived in 1598 as envoys from the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Earl of Essex</a> on an unofficial mission to induce Persia into an anti-Ottoman alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> As mentioned by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_Iranica" title="Encyclopaedia Iranica">Encyclopaedia Iranica</a></i>, lastly, from 1600 onwards, the Safavid statesman <a href="/wiki/Allahverdi_Khan" title="Allahverdi Khan">Allāhverdī Khan</a>, in conjunction with Robert Sherley, undertook further reorganizations of the army, which meant among other things further dramatically increasing the number of <i>ghulams</i> to 25,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALL.C4.80HVERD.C4.AA_KHAN_1_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALL.C4.80HVERD.C4.AA_KHAN_1-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abbas also moved the capital to <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, deeper into central Iran. Abbas I built a new city next to the ancient Persian one. From this time the state began to take on a more Persian character. The Safavids ultimately succeeded in establishing a new Persian national monarchy.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recovery_of_territory_from_the_Uzbeks_and_the_Ottomans">Recovery of territory from the Uzbeks and the Ottomans</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1603%E2%80%9318)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1603–18)">Ottoman–Safavid War (1603–18)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="447" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg/170px-Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg/255px-Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg/340px-Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abbas_I_of_Persia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abbas I as shown on one of the paintings in the <a href="/wiki/Chehel_Sotoun" title="Chehel Sotoun">Chehel Sotoun</a> pavilion.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abbas I first fought the Uzbeks, recapturing <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> and Mashhad in 1598. Then he turned against Persia's archrival, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, recapturing <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasian</a> provinces by 1616, all through the 1603-1618, marking the first grand Safavid pitched victory over the Ottomans. He also used his new force to dislodge the Portuguese from <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> (1602) and, with English help, from <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">Hormuz</a> (1622), in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> (a vital link in Portuguese trade with India). He expanded commercial links with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_East_India_Company" title="English East India Company">English East India Company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a>. Thus Abbas was able to break the dependence on the Qizilbash for military might indefinitely and therefore was able to centralize control, for the first time since fully the foundation of the Safavid state.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> and Safavids fought over the fertile plains of Iraq for more than 150 years. The capture of Baghdad by Ismail I in 1509 was only followed by its loss to the Ottoman Sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suleiman_I" title="Suleiman I">Suleiman I</a> in 1534. After subsequent campaigns, the Safavids <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capture_of_Baghdad_(1623)" title="Capture of Baghdad (1623)">recaptured Baghdad in 1623</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1623%E2%80%9339)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)">Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)</a> yet lost it again to <a href="/wiki/Murad_IV" title="Murad IV">Murad IV</a> in 1638 after Abbas had died. Henceforth a treaty, signed in <a href="/wiki/Qasr-e_Shirin" title="Qasr-e Shirin">Qasr-e Shirin</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a> was established delineating a border between Iran and Turkey in 1639, a border which still stands in northwest Iran/southeast Turkey. The 150-year tug-of-war accentuated the Sunni and Shi'a rift in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quelling_the_Georgian_uprising">Quelling the Georgian uprising</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capture_of_Tbilisi_and_G%C3%B6k%C3%A7e_war" title="Capture of Tbilisi and Gökçe war">Capture of Tbilisi and Gökçe war</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3904" data-file-width="1775" height="374" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif/170px-%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif/255px-%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif/340px-%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E1%83%A0%E1%83%9D%E1%83%A1%E1%83%A2%E1%83%9D%E1%83%9B_%E1%83%AE%E1%83%90%E1%83%9C%E1%83%98.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rostom_of_Kartli" title="Rostom of Kartli">Rostom</a> (also known as <i>Rustam Khan</i>), viceroy of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kartli" title="Kingdom of Kartli">Kartli</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, from 1633-1658.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1614–16 during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman-Safavid_War_(1603-1618)" title="Ottoman-Safavid War (1603-1618)">Ottoman-Safavid War (1603-1618)</a>, Abbas suppressed a rebellion led by his formerly most loyal <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> subjects <a href="/wiki/Luarsab_II_of_Kartli" title="Luarsab II of Kartli">Luarsab II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teimuraz_I_of_Kakheti" title="Teimuraz I of Kakheti">Teimuraz I</a> (also known as <i>Tahmuras Khan</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kakheti" title="Kingdom of Kakheti">Kingdom of Kakheti</a>. Several years earlier, in 1613, Abbas had appointed these trusted Georgian <i>gholams</i> of his on the puppet thrones of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kartli" title="Kingdom of Kartli">Kartli</a> and Kakheti, the Iranian Safavid ruled areas of Georgia. Later that year, when the shah summoned them to join him on a hunting expedition in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazandaran_province" title="Mazandaran province">Mazandaran</a>, they didn't show up due to the fear they would be either imprisoned or killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69-128">[128]</a></sup> Ultimately forming an alliance, the two sought refuge with the Ottoman forces in Ottoman ruled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Imereti" title="Kingdom of Imereti">Imereti</a>. This defection of two of the shah's most trusted subjects and <i>gholams</i> infuriated the shah, as reported by the Safavid court historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iskander_Beg_Munshi" title="Iskander Beg Munshi">Iskander Beg Munshi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69-128">[128]</a></sup> The following spring in 1614, Abbas I appointed a grandson of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Imereti" title="Alexander II of Imereti">Alexander II of Imereti</a> to the throne of Kartli, <a href="/wiki/Jesse_of_Kakheti" title="Jesse of Kakheti">Jesse of Kakheti</a> also known as "Isā Khān".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69-128">[128]</a></sup> Raised up at the court in <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Muslim</a>, he was fully loyal to the shah. Subsequently, the shah marched upon <a href="/wiki/Kutaisi" title="Kutaisi">Grem</a>, the capital of Imereti, and punished its peoples for harbouring his defected subjects. He returned to Kartli, and in two punitive campaigns he <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I%27s_Kakhetian_and_Kartlian_campaigns" title="Abbas I's Kakhetian and Kartlian campaigns">devastated Tblisi</a>, killed 60–70,000 Kakheti Georgian peasants, and deported between 130,000-200,000 <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> captives to mainland Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMikaberidze2015291.2C_536_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMikaberidze2015291.2C_536-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> After having fully secured the region, he executed the rebellious <a href="/wiki/Luarsab_II_of_Kartli" title="Luarsab II of Kartli">Luarsab II of Kartli</a> and later had the Georgian queen <a href="/wiki/Ketevan_the_Martyr" title="Ketevan the Martyr">Ketevan</a>, who had been sent to the shah as negotiator, tortured to death when she refused to renounce Christianity, in an act of revenge for the recalcitrance of Teimuraz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Kakheti lost two-thirds of its population in these years by Abbas' punitive campaign due to being either deported to Iran like the majority were, or to a lesser extent being slaughtered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Teimuraz did return to eastern Georgia in 1615 and defeated there a Safavid force. However it was just a setback, as Abbas had already been making long-term plans for such a thing never to happen again, for he was (eventually successfully) making the eastern Georgian territories an integral part of the Safavid provinces; in 1619 he appointed the loyal <a href="/wiki/Simon_II_of_Kartli" title="Simon II of Kartli">Simon II</a> (or <i>Semayun Khan</i>) on the symbolic throne of Kakheti, while placing a series of his own governors to rule of districts where rebellious inhabitants were mostly located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Colin_P._Mitchell_p_69-128">[128]</a></sup> Moreover, he planned to deport all nobles of Kartli.</p>
<p>After the events which ended in 1616, Iranian rule had been fully restored over eastern Georgia, but the Georgian territories would continue to produce resistance to Safavid enroachments from 1624 until Abbas' death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suppressing_the_Kurdish_rebellion">Suppressing the Kurdish rebellion</span></h4>
<p>In 1609–10, a war broke out between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> tribes and the Safavid Empire. After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_DimDim" title="Battle of DimDim">a long and bloody siege</a> led by the Safavid grand vizier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hatim_Bek_Urdubadi" title="Hatim Bek Urdubadi">Hatem Beg</a>, which lasted from November 1609 to the summer of 1610, the Kurdish stronghold of Dimdim was captured. Shah Abbas ordered a general massacre in Beradost and Mukriyan (<a href="/wiki/Mahabad" title="Mahabad">Mahabad</a>, reported by Eskandar Beg Monshi, Safavid Historian (1557–1642), in "Alam Ara Abbasi") and resettled the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afshar_tribe" title="Afshar tribe">Afshar tribe</a> in the region while deporting many Kurdish tribes to <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Nowadays, there is a community of nearly 1.7 million people who are descendants of the tribes deported from Kurdistan to Khorasan (Northeastern Iran) by the Safavids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contacts_with_Europe_during_Abbas.27s_reign">Contacts with Europe during Abbas's reign</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="531" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg/170px-Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg/255px-Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg/340px-Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Husain_Ali_Beg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ambassador <a href="/wiki/Hossein_Ali_Beg_Bayat" title="Hossein Ali Beg Bayat">Husain Ali Beg</a> led the first <a href="/wiki/Persian_embassy_to_Europe_(1599%E2%80%931602)" title="Persian embassy to Europe (1599–1602)">Persian embassy to Europe (1599–1602)</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abbas's tolerance towards Christians was part of his policy of establishing diplomatic links with European powers to try to enlist their help in the fight against their common enemy, the Ottoman Empire. The idea of such an anti-Ottoman alliance was not a new one—over a century before, <a href="/wiki/Uzun_Hassan" title="Uzun Hassan">Uzun Hassan</a>, then ruler of part of Iran, had asked the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetians</a> for military aid—but none of the Safavids had made diplomatic overtures to Europe. Shah Ismail I was the first of the Safavids that tried to establish once again an alliance against the common Ottoman enemy through the earlier stages of the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg%E2%80%93Persian_alliance" title="Habsburg–Persian alliance">Habsburg–Persian alliance</a>, but this also proved to be largely unfruitful during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Abbas's attitude however was in marked contrast to that of his grandfather, Tahmasp I, who had expelled the English traveller <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Jenkinson" title="Anthony Jenkinson">Anthony Jenkinson</a> from his court on hearing he was a Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> For his part, Abbas declared that he "preferred the dust from the shoe soles of the lowest Christian to the highest Ottoman personage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Abbas would take active and all measures needed in order to get the alliances done.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Embassy_to_Europe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1496" data-file-width="2130" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Embassy_to_Europe.jpg/220px-Embassy_to_Europe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Embassy_to_Europe.jpg/330px-Embassy_to_Europe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Embassy_to_Europe.jpg/440px-Embassy_to_Europe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embassy_to_Europe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fresco in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doge%27s_Palace,_Venice" title="Doge's Palace, Venice">Doge's Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> depicting <a href="/wiki/Marino_Grimani_(doge)" title="Marino Grimani (doge)">Doge Marino Grimani</a> receiving the Persian Ambassadors, 1599</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1599, Abbas sent his <a href="/wiki/Persian_embassy_to_Europe_(1599%E2%80%931602)" title="Persian embassy to Europe (1599–1602)">first diplomatic mission to Europe</a>. The group crossed the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> and spent the winter in Moscow, before proceeding through Norway, Germany (where it was received by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Rudolf_II" title="Emperor Rudolf II">Emperor Rudolf II</a>) to Rome where <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VIII" title="Pope Clement VIII">Pope Clement VIII</a> gave the travellers a long audience. They finally arrived at the court of <a href="/wiki/Philip_III_of_Spain" title="Philip III of Spain">Philip III of Spain</a> in 1602. Although the expedition never managed to return to Iran, being shipwrecked on the journey around Africa, it marked an important new step in contacts between Iran and Europe. The Europeans began to be fascinated by the Iranians and their culture—Shakespeare's 1601–2 <i><a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Night" title="Twelfth Night">Twelfth Night</a></i>, for example, makes two references (at II.5 and III.4) to 'the Sophy', then the English term for the Shahs of Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Henceforward, the number of diplomatic missions to and fro greatly increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2652" data-file-width="2100" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg/220px-Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg/330px-Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg/440px-Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abbas_I_as_a_new_Caesar_being_honoured_by_the_trumpets_of_fame_and_the_Persian_embassy_in_Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_by_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Abbas I as a new <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a> being honoured by the Trumpets of Fame, together with the <a href="/wiki/Persian_embassy_to_Europe_(1609%E2%80%9315)" title="Persian embassy to Europe (1609–15)">1609-1615 Persian embassy</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/File:Allegorie_de_l_Occasion_Frans_II_Francken_1628.jpg" title="File:Allegorie de l Occasion Frans II Francken 1628.jpg">Allégorie de l'Occasion</a></i>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frans_II_Francken" title="Frans II Francken">Frans II Francken</a>, 1628.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The shah had set great store on an alliance with Spain, the chief opponent of the Ottomans in Europe. Abbas offered trading rights and the chance to preach Christianity in Iran in return for help against the Ottomans. But the stumbling block of Hormuz remained, a vassal kingdom which had fallen into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Habsburgs" title="Spanish Habsburgs">Spanish Habsburgs</a>' hands when the King of Spain inherited the throne of Portugal in 1580. The Spanish demanded Abbas break off relations with the English before they would consider relinquishing the town. Abbas was unable to comply. Eventually Abbas became frustrated with Spain, as he did with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, which wanted him to make his over 400,000 <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a> subjects swear allegiance to the Pope but did not trouble to inform the shah when the <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Rudolf II, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Rudolf</a> signed a peace treaty with the Ottomans. Contacts with the Pope, Poland and <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Moscow</a> were no more fruitful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>More came of Abbas's contacts with the English, although England had little interest in fighting against the Ottomans. The Sherley brothers arrived in 1598 and helped reorganize the Iranian army, which proved to be crucial in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1603%E2%80%9318)" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1603–18)">Ottoman–Safavid War (1603–18)</a>, which resulted in Ottoman defeats in all stages of the war and the first clear pitched Safavid victory of their archrivals. One of the Shirley brothers, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shirley" title="Robert Shirley">Robert Shirley</a>, would lead Abbas's <a href="/wiki/Persian_embassy_to_Europe_(1609%E2%80%9315)" title="Persian embassy to Europe (1609–15)">second diplomatic mission to Europe</a> from 1609-1615.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The English at sea, represented by the English East India Company, also began to take an interest in Iran and in 1622 four of its ships helped Abbas retake Hormuz from the Portuguese in the <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Ormuz_(1622)" title="Capture of Ormuz (1622)">Capture of Ormuz (1622)</a>. It was the beginning of the East India Company's long-running interest in Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_and_legacy_of_Abbas_I">Succession and legacy of Abbas I</span></h4>
<p>Due to his obsessive fear of assassination, Shah Abbas either put to death or blinded any member of his family who aroused his suspicion. One of his sons, his oldest son and the crown prince <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Baqer_Mirza" title="Mohammad Baqer Mirza">Mohammad Baqer Mirza</a>, was executed following a court intrigue in which several Circassians were involved, while two others were blinded. Since two other sons had predeceased him, the result was a personal tragedy for Shah Abbas. When he died on 19 January 1629, he had no son capable of succeeding him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The beginning of the 17th century saw the power of the Qizilbash drastically diminish, the original militia that had helped Ismail I capture Tabriz and which had gained many administrative powers over the centuries. Power was fully shifting to the new class of <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasian</a> deportees and imports, many of the hundreds of thousands ethnic <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>. This new society layer would continue to play a vital role in Iranian history up to including the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar dynasty</a>, some 300 years after Abbas' death.</p>
<p>At its zenith, during the long reign of Shah Abbas I the empire's reach comprised <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabardino-Balkaria" title="Kabardino-Balkaria">Kabardino-Balkaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_the_Safavid_state">Decline of the Safavid state</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotaki_dynasty" title="Hotaki dynasty">Hotaki dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afsharid_dynasty" title="Afsharid dynasty">Afsharid dynasty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1722-1723)" title="Russo-Persian War (1722-1723)">Russo-Persian War (1722-1723)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Constantinople_(1724)" title="Treaty of Constantinople (1724)">Treaty of Constantinople (1724)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Abbas_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="1024" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/220px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/330px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Shah_Abbas_II.jpg/440px-Shah_Abbas_II.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Abbas_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Abbas_II_of_Persia" title="Abbas II of Persia">Shah Abbas the II</a> holding a banquet for foreign dignitaries. Detail from a ceiling fresco at the <a href="/wiki/Chehel_Sotoun" title="Chehel Sotoun">Chehel Sotoun Palace</a> in Isfahan.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to fighting its perennial enemies, their archrival the Ottomans and the Uzbeks as the 17th century progressed, Iran had to contend with the rise of new neighbors. Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscovy" title="Muscovy">Muscovy</a> in the previous century had deposed two western Asian khanates of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> and expanded its influence into Europe, the Caucasus Mountains and Central Asia. <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan" title="Astrakhan">Astrakhan</a> came under Russian rule, nearing the Safavid possessions in <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a>. In the far eastern territories, the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a> of India had expanded into <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>) at the expense of Iranian control, briefly taking <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E1%83%97%E1%83%94%E1%83%98%E1%83%9B%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%90%E1%83%96_I.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="304" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/%E1%83%97%E1%83%94%E1%83%98%E1%83%9B%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%90%E1%83%96_I.png/220px-%E1%83%97%E1%83%94%E1%83%98%E1%83%9B%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%90%E1%83%96_I.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/%E1%83%97%E1%83%94%E1%83%98%E1%83%9B%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%90%E1%83%96_I.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E1%83%97%E1%83%94%E1%83%98%E1%83%9B%E1%83%A3%E1%83%A0%E1%83%90%E1%83%96_I.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Teimuraz_I_of_Kakheti" title="Teimuraz I of Kakheti">Teimuraz I of Kakheti</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1659, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kakheti" title="Kingdom of Kakheti">Kingdom of Kakheti</a> rose up against the Safavid Iranian rule due to a change of policy that included the mass settling of <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> Turkic tribes in the region in order to repopulate the province, after Shah Abbas' <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I%27s_Kakhetian_and_Kartlian_campaigns" title="Abbas I's Kakhetian and Kartlian campaigns">earlier</a> mass deportations of between 130,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> - 200,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMikaberidze2015291.2C_536_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMikaberidze2015291.2C_536-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">Georgian</a> subjects to Iran's mainland and massacre of another thousand in 1616 virtually left the province without any even remotely substantial amount of population. This <a href="/wiki/Bakhtrioni_Uprising" title="Bakhtrioni Uprising">Bakhtrioni Uprising</a> was successfully defeated under personal direction of Shah <a href="/wiki/Abbas_II_of_Persia" title="Abbas II of Persia">Abbas II</a> himself. However, strategically it remained inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The Iranian authority was restored in Kakheti, but the Qizilbash Turkics were prevented from settling in Kakheti, which undermined the planned Iranian policies in the respective province.</p>
<p>More importantly, the Dutch East India company and later English/British used their superior means of maritime power to control trade routes in the western Indian Ocean. As a result, Iran was cut off from overseas links to East Africa, the Arabian peninsula, and South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Overland trade grew notably however, as Iran was able to further develop its overland trade with North and Central Europe during the second half of the seventeenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> In the late seventeenth century, Iranian merchants established a permanent presence as far north as Narva on the Baltic sea, in what now is Estonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Dutch and English were still able to drain the Iranian government of much of its precious metal supplies. Except for Shah Abbas II, the Safavid rulers after Abbas I were therefore rendered ineffectual, and the Iranian government declined and finally collapsed when a serious military threat emerged on its eastern border in the early eighteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The end of the reign of Abbas II, 1666, thus marked the beginning of the end of the Safavid dynasty. Despite falling revenues and military threats, later shahs had lavish lifestyles. <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Husayn" title="Sultan Husayn">Sultan Husayn</a> (1694–1722) in particular was known for his love of wine and disinterest in governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="1992" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg/220px-Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg/330px-Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg/440px-Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jomann_Imperium_Periscum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Persia, c. 1700 by Johann Baptist Homann (1644–1724)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The country was repeatedly raided on its frontiers—Kerman by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baloch_tribes" title="Baloch tribes">Baloch tribes</a> in 1698, Khorasan by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotaki_dynasty" title="Hotaki dynasty">Hotakis</a> in 1717, <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Shirvan_Beylarbeylik" title="Shirvan Beylarbeylik">Shirvan</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Lezgins" title="Lezgins">Lezgins</a> <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Shamakhi_(1721)" title="Sack of Shamakhi (1721)">in 1721</a>, constantly in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> by Sunni peninsula Arabs. Sultan Hosein tried to forcibly convert his Afghan subjects in Qandahar from Sunni to the Shi'a sect of Islam. In response, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghilzai" title="Ghilzai">Ghilzai</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afghan_(name)" title="Afghan (name)">Afghan</a> chieftain named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mir_Wais_Hotak" title="Mir Wais Hotak">Mir Wais Hotak</a> revolted and killed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurgin_Khan" title="Gurgin Khan">Gurgin Khan</a>, the Safavid governor of the region, along with his army. In 1722, an Afghan army led by Mir Wais' son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mir_Mahmud_Hotaki" title="Mir Mahmud Hotaki">Mahmud</a> advanced on the heart of the empire and defeated the government forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gulnabad" title="Battle of Gulnabad">Battle of Gulnabad</a>. He then <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Isfahan" title="Siege of Isfahan">besieged</a> the capital of Isfahan, until Shah Sultan Husayn <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">abdicated</a> and acknowledged him as the new king of Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A more detailed citation is required. (June 2015)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> At the same time, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">Russians</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Great" title="Peter the Great">Peter the Great</a> attacked and conquered swaths of Safavid Iran's <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasian</a>, and northern mainland territories through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Persian_War_(1722-1723)" title="Russo-Persian War (1722-1723)">Russo-Persian War (1722-1723)</a>. The Safavids' archrivals, the neighbouring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a>, invaded western and northwestern Safavid Iran and took swaths of territory there, including the city of Baghdad. Together with the Russians, they agreed to divide and keep the conquered Iranian territories for themselves as confirmed in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Constantinople_(1724)" title="Treaty of Constantinople (1724)">Treaty of Constantinople (1724)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="467" data-file-width="489" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG/220px-Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG/330px-Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG/440px-Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moll_1720_Persian_Empire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of Safavid Empire in 1720, showing different states of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The tribal Afghans rode roughshod over their conquered territory for seven years but were prevented from making further gains by <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a>, a former slave who had risen to military leadership within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afshar_tribe" title="Afshar tribe">Afshar tribe</a> in Khorasan, a vassal state of the Safavids. Quickly making name as a military genius both feared and respected amongst its friends and enemies (including Persia's archrival the Ottoman Empire, and Russia; both empires Nader would deal with soon afterwards), Nader Shah easily defeated the Ghilzai Hotaki forces in the 1729 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Damghan_(1729)" title="Battle of Damghan (1729)">Battle of Damghan</a>. He had removed them from power and banished them out of Persia by 1729. In 1732 by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Resht" title="Treaty of Resht">Treaty of Resht</a> and in 1735 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ganja" title="Treaty of Ganja">Treaty of Ganja</a>, he negotiated an agreement with the government of Empress <a href="/wiki/Anna_of_Russia" title="Anna of Russia">Anna Ioanovna</a> for them to cede back the recently annexed Iranian territories, making most of the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> fall back into Iranian hands, while establishing an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iran-Russia_relations" title="Iran-Russia relations">Irano-Russian</a> alliance against the common neighbouring Ottoman enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Persian_War_(1730%E2%80%9335)" title="Ottoman–Persian War (1730–35)">Ottoman–Persian War (1730–35)</a>, he retook all territories lost by the Ottoman invasion of the 1720s, as well as beyond. With the Safavid state and its territories secured, in 1738 Nader conquered the Hotaki's last stronghold in Qandahar; in the same year, in need of fortune to aid his military careers against his Ottoman and Russian imperial rivals, he started his invasion of the wealthy but weak Mughal Empire accompanied by his Georgian subject <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erekle_II" title="Erekle II">Erekle II</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> occupying <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nader_Shah%27s_invasion_of_India" title="Nader Shah's invasion of India">as far as Delhi</a>, in India, when he completely humiliated and looted the military inferior Mughals. These cities were later inherited by his <a href="/wiki/Durrani" title="Durrani">Abdali</a> Afghan military commander, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Durrani" title="Ahmad Shah Durrani">Ahmad Shah Durrani</a>. Nadir had effective control under Shah <a href="/wiki/Tahmasp_II" title="Tahmasp II">Tahmasp II</a> and then ruled as regent of the infant <a href="/wiki/Abbas_III" title="Abbas III">Abbas III</a> until 1736 when he had himself crowned shah.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="969" data-file-width="1195" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg/220px-Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg/330px-Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg/440px-Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Safavid_Persian_Empire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part of the Safavid Persian Empire (on right), the Ottoman Empire, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a> in general. Emanuel Bowen, 1744–52.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Immediately after Nader Shah's assassination in 1747 and the disintegration of his short-lived empire, the Safavids were re-appointed as shahs of Iran in order to lend legitimacy to the nascent <a href="/wiki/Zand_dynasty" title="Zand dynasty">Zand dynasty</a>. However the brief puppet regime of <a href="/wiki/Ismail_III" title="Ismail III">Ismail III</a> ended in 1760 when <a href="/wiki/Karim_Khan" title="Karim Khan">Karim Khan</a> felt strong enough to take nominal power of the country as well and officially end the Safavid dynasty.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shia_Islam_as_the_state_religion">Shia Islam as the state religion</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_from_Sunnism_to_Shiism" title="Safavid conversion of Iran from Sunnism to Shiism">Safavid conversion of Iran from Sunnism to Shiism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="640" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg/220px-Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg/330px-Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg/440px-Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_soleiman_safavi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shah Suleiman I and his courtiers, Isfahan, 1670. Painter is <a href="/wiki/Aliquli_Jabbadar" title="Aliquli Jabbadar">Aliquli Jabbadar</a>, and is kept at The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a> Institute of Oriental Studies in Russia, ever since it was acquired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsar_Nicholas_II" title="Tsar Nicholas II">Tsar Nicholas II</a>. Note the two <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgian</a> figures with their names at the top left.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even though the Safavids were not the first Shia rulers in Iran, they played a crucial role in making Shia Islam the official religion in the whole of Iran, as well as what is nowadays the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Azerbaijan" title="Republic of Azerbaijan">Republic of Azerbaijan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> There were large Shia communities in some cities like <a href="/wiki/Qom" title="Qom">Qom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sabzevar" title="Sabzevar">Sabzevar</a> as early as the 8th century. In the 10th and 11th centuries the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buwayhid" title="Buwayhid">Buwayhids</a>, who were of the <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidiyyah</a> branch of Shia, ruled in <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isfahan_(city)" title="Isfahan (city)">Isfahan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. As a result of the Mongol conquest and the relative religious tolerance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilkhanids" title="Ilkhanids">Ilkhanids</a>, Shia dynasties were re-established in Iran, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarbedaran" title="Sarbedaran">Sarbedaran</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a> being the most important. The Ilkhanid ruler <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljait%C3%BC" title="Öljaitü">Öljaitü</a> converted to Twelver Shiism in the 13th century.</p>
<p>Following his conquest of Iran and Azerbaijan, Ismail I made conversion mandatory for the largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> population. The Sunni <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">Ulema</a> or clergy were either killed or exiled. Ismail I, brought in mainstream <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Ithnā'ashariyyah</a> Shi'a religious leaders and granted them land and money in return for loyalty. Later, during the Safavid and especially <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Qajar</a> period, the Shia Ulema's power increased and they were able to exercise a role, independent of or compatible with the government.</p>
<p>Iran became a feudal theocracy: the Shah was held to be the divinely ordained head of state and religion. In the following centuries, this religious stance cemented both Iran's internal cohesion and national feelings and provoked attacks by its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> neighbors, most notably its neighbouring rival, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_and_the_role_of_Qizilbash">Military and the role of Qizilbash</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Safavid-helmet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1348" data-file-width="1000" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Safavid-helmet.jpg/170px-Safavid-helmet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Safavid-helmet.jpg/255px-Safavid-helmet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Safavid-helmet.jpg/340px-Safavid-helmet.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Safavid-helmet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Safavid helmet</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Qizilbash were a wide variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27ite" title="Shi'ite">Shi'ite</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Ghulat" title="Ghulat">ghulāt</a></i>) and mostly <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">Turcoman</a> militant groups who helped found the Safavid Empire. Their military power was essential during the reign of the Shahs Ismail and Tahmasp. The Qizilbash tribes were essential to the military of Iran until the rule of <a href="/wiki/Abbas_I_of_Persia" title="Abbas I of Persia">Shah Abbas I</a>- their leaders were able to exercise enormous influence and participate in court intrigues (assassinating Shah <a href="/wiki/Ismail_II" title="Ismail II">Ismail II</a> for example).</p>
<p>A major problem faced by <a href="/wiki/Ismail_I" title="Ismail I">Ismail I</a> after the establishment of the Safavid state was how to bridge the gap between the two major ethnic groups in that state: the <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a> ("Redhead") Turcomans, the "men of sword" of classical Islamic society whose military prowess had brought him to power, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> elements, the "men of the pen", who filled the ranks of the bureaucracy and the religious establishment in the Safavid state as they had done for centuries under previous rulers of Persia, be they <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" title="Turkmen people">Turkmens</a>. As <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Minorsky" title="Vladimir Minorsky">Vladimir Minorsky</a> put it, friction between these two groups was inevitable, because the Qizilbash "were no party to the national Persian tradition".</p>
<p>Between 1508 and 1524, the year of Ismail's death, the shah appointed five successive Persians to the office of <i>vakil</i>. When the second Persian vakil was placed in command of a Safavid army in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, the Qizilbash, considering it a dishonor to be obliged to serve under him, deserted him on the battlefield with the result that he was slain. The fourth vakil was murdered by the Qizilbash, and the fifth was put to death by them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ismailsafaviiranica_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-ismailsafaviiranica-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms_in_the_military">Reforms in the military</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Persian_Musketeer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="857" data-file-width="445" height="424" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Persian_Musketeer.jpg/220px-Persian_Musketeer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Persian_Musketeer.jpg/330px-Persian_Musketeer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Persian_Musketeer.jpg/440px-Persian_Musketeer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Persian_Musketeer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian Musketeer in time of Abbas I by Habib-Allah Mashadi after Falsafi (Berlin Museum of Islamic Art).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shah Abbas realized that in order to retain absolute control over his empire without antagonizing the Qizilbash, he needed to create reforms that reduced the dependency that the shah had on their military support. Part of these reforms was the creation of <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty#Society" title="Safavid dynasty">the 3rd force</a> within the aristocracy and all other functions within the empire, but even more important in undermining the authority of the Qizilbash was the introduction of the Royal Corps into the military. This military force would serve the shah only and eventually consisted of four separate branches:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Shahsevans: these were 12,000 strong and built up from the small group of <i>qurchis</i> that Shah Abbas had inherited from his predecessor. The <a href="/wiki/Shahsevan" title="Shahsevan">Shahsevans</a>, or "Friends of the King", were Qizilbash tribesmen who had forsaken their tribal allegiance for allegiance to the shah alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ghulams: Tahmasp I had started introducing huge amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgian_people" title="Georgian people">Georgian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a> slaves and deportees from the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, of whom a sizeable amount would become part of the future ghulam system. Shah Abbas expanded this program significantly and fully implemented it, and eventually created a force of 15,000 ghulam cavalrymen and 3,000 ghulam royal bodyguards. With the advent of the brother's Shirley at Abbas' court and by the efforts of statesman <a href="/wiki/Allahverdi_Khan" title="Allahverdi Khan">Allahverdi Khan</a>, from 1600 onwards, the ghulam fighting regiments were further dramatically expanded under Abbas reaching 25,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALL.C4.80HVERD.C4.AA_KHAN_1_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-ALL.C4.80HVERD.C4.AA_KHAN_1-127">[127]</a></sup> Under Abbas, this force amounted to a total of near 40,000 soldiers paid for and beholden to the Shah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savory_1980_79_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Savory_1980_79-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BN142_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-BN142-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> They would become the elite soldiers of the Safavid armies (like the Ottoman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jannisary" title="Jannisary">Jannisary</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iranicaonline.org_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-iranicaonline.org-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>Musketers: realizing the advantages that the Ottomans had because of their firearms, Shah Abbas was at pains to equip both the qurchi and the ghulam soldiers with up-to-date weaponry. More importantly, for the first time in Iranian history, a substantial infantry corps of musketeers (<i>tofang-chis</i>), numbering 12 000, was created.</li>
<li>Artillery Corps: with the help of Westerners, he also formed an artillery corps of 12 000 men, although this was the weakest element in his army. According to <a href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Herbert,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Thomas Herbert, 1st Baronet">Sir Thomas Herbert</a>, who accompanied the British embassy to Persia in 1628, the Persians relied heavily on support from the Europeans in manufacturing cannons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blow_38_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blow_38-166">[166]</a></sup> It wasn't until a century later, when <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> became the Commander in Chief of the military that sufficient effort was put into modernizing the artillery corps and the Persians managed to excel and become self-sufficient in the manufacturing of firearms.</li>
</ul>
<p>Despite the reforms, the Qizilbash would remain the strongest and most effective element within the military, accounting for more than half of its total strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blow_38_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blow_38-166">[166]</a></sup> But the creation of this large standing army, that, for the first time in Safavid history, was serving directly under the Shah, significantly reduced their influence, and perhaps any possibilities for the type of civil unrest that had caused havoc during the reign of the previous shahs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span></h2>
<p>A proper term for the Safavid society is what we today can call a <i><a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a></i>, meaning a society in which officials were appointed on the basis of worth and merit, and not on the basis of birth. It was certainly not an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>, nor was it an <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>. Sons of nobles were considered for the succession of their fathers as a mark of respect, but they had to prove themselves worthy of the position. This system avoided an entrenched aristocracy or a cast society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> There even are numerous recorded accounts of laymen that rose to high official posts, as a result of their merits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, the Iranian society during the Safavids was that of a hierarchy, with the Shah at the apex of the hierarchical pyramid, the common people, merchants and peasants at the base, and the aristocrats in between. The term <i>dowlat</i>, which in modern Persian means "government", was then an abstract term meaning "bliss" or "felicity", and it began to be used as concrete sense of the Safavid state, reflecting the view that the people had of their ruler, as someone elevated above humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savory_177_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savory_177-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also among the aristocracy, in the middle of the hierarchical pyramid, were the religious officials, who, mindful of the historic role of the religious classes as a buffer between the ruler and his subjects, usually did their best to shield the ordinary people from oppressive governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savory_177_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Savory_177-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_customs_and_culture_of_the_people">The customs and culture of the people</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jean_Chardin" title="Jean Chardin">Jean Chardin</a> devoted a whole chapter in his book to describing the Persian character, which apparently fascinated him greatly. As he spent a large bulk of his life in Persia, he involved himself in, and took part in, their everyday rituals and habits, and eventually acquired intimate knowledge of their culture, customs and character. He admired their consideration towards foreigners, but he also stumbled upon characteristics that he found challenging. His descriptions of the public appearance, clothes and customs are corroborated by the miniatures, drawings and paintings from that time which have survived. As he describes them:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferrier_110_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrier_110-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Their imagination is animated, quick and fruitful. Their memory is free and prolific. They are very favorably drawn to the sciences, the liberal and mechanical arts. Their temperament is open and leans towards sensual pleasure and self-indulgence, which makes them pay little attention to economy or business.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>He then goes on:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferrier_110_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrier_110-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>They are very philosophical over the good and bad things in life and about expectations for the future. They are little tainted with avarice, desiring only to acquire in order to spend. They love to enjoy what is to hand and they refuse nothing which contributes to it, having no anxiety about the future which they leave to providence and fate.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>But as he also experienced:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...the Persians are dissembling, shamelessly deceitful and the greatest flatterers in the world, using great deception and insolence. They lack good faith in business dealings, in which they cheat so adeptly that one is always taken in. Hypocrisy is the usual disguise in which they proceed. They say their prayers and perform their rituals in the most devout manner. They hold the wisest and most pious conversation of which they are capable. And although they are naturally inclined to humanity, hospitality, mercy and other worldly goods, nevertheless, they do not cease feigning in order to give the semblance of being much better than they really are.</p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Character">Character</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="459" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg/170px-Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg/255px-Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg/340px-Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Robert_Shirley_by_Anthony_Van_Dyck_1622_Rome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Anthony_Shirley" title="Anthony Shirley">Anthony Shirley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shirley" title="Robert Shirley">Robert Shirley</a> (<i>pictured in 1622</i>) helped modernize the Persian Army.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is however no question, from reading Chardin's descriptions of their manners, that he considered them to be a well-educated and well-behaved people, who certainly knew the strict etiquettes of social intercourse. As he describes them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The Persians are the most civilized of the peoples of the East, and what the French are to Europe, they are to the Orient... Their bearing and countenance is the best-composed, mild, serious, impressive, genial and welcoming as far as possible. They never fail to perform at once the appropriate gestures of politeness when meeting each other... They are the most wheedling people in the world, with the most engaging manners, the most supple spirits and a language that is gentle and flattering, and devoid of unpleasant terms but rather full of circumlocutions.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Unlike Europeans, they much disliked physical activity, and were not in favor of exercise for its own sake, preferring the leisure of repose and luxuries that life could offer. Travelling was valued only for the specific purpose of getting from one place to another, not interesting them self in seeing new places and experiencing different cultures. It was perhaps this sort of attitude towards the rest of the world that accounted for the ignorance of Persians regarding other countries of the world. The exercises that they took part in were for keeping the body supple and sturdy and to acquire skills in handling of arms. <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">Archery</a> took first place. Second place was held by <a href="/wiki/Fencing" title="Fencing">fencing</a>, where the wrist had to be firm but flexible and movements agile. Thirdly there was horsemanship. A very strenuous form of exercise which the Persians greatly enjoyed was hunting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">Entertainment</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="933" data-file-width="600" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg/170px-Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg/255px-Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg/340px-Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polo_game_from_poem_Guy_u_Chawgan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">persian miniature</a> depicting a polo-match</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since pre-Islamic times, the sport of <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a> had been an integral part of the Iranian identity, and the professional wrestlers, who performed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zurkhaneh" title="Zurkhaneh">Zurkhanehs</a>, were considered important members of the society. Each town had their own troop of wrestlers, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varzesh-e_Pahlavani" title="Varzesh-e Pahlavani">Pahlavans</a>. Their sport also provided the masses with entertainment and spectacle. Chardin described one such event:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">The two wrestlers were covered in grease. They are present on the level ground, and a small drum is always playing during the contest for excitement. They swear to a good fight and shake hands. That done, they slap their thighs, buttocks and hips to the rhythm of the drum. That is for the women and to get themselves in good form. After that they join together in uttering a great cry and trying to overthrow each other.</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>As well as wrestling, what gathered the masses was fencing, tightrope dancers, puppet-players and acrobats, performing in large squares, such as the <a href="/wiki/Naqsh-e_Jahan_Square" title="Naqsh-e Jahan Square">Royal square</a>. A leisurely form of amusement was to be found in the <a href="/wiki/Cabaret" title="Cabaret">cabarets</a>, particularly in certain districts, like those near the mausoleum of Harun-e Velayat. People met there to drink liqueurs or coffee, to smoke tobacco or opium, and to chat or listen to poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothes_and_Appearances">Clothes and Appearances</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="579" data-file-width="1116" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg/220px-74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg/330px-74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg/440px-74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:74_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_women_customs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lady's clothing in the 1600s</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="411" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg/220px-73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg/330px-73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:73_Chardin_Safavid_Persia_men_customs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Men's clothing in the 1600s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As noted before, a key aspect of the Persian character was its love of luxury, particularly on keeping up appearances. They would adorn their clothes, wearing stones and decorate the harness of their horses. Men wore many rings on their fingers, almost as many as their wives. They also placed jewels on their arms, such as on daggers and swords. Daggers were worn at the waist. In describing the lady's clothing, he noted that Persian dress revealed more of the figure than did the European, but that women appeared differently depending on whether they were at home in the presence of friends and family, or if they were in the public. In private they usually wore a veil that only covered the hair and the back, but upon leaving the home, they put on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manteaus" title="Manteaus">manteaus</a></i>, large cloaks that concealed their whole bodies except their faces. They often dyed their feet and hands with <a href="/wiki/Henna#Traditions_of_henna_as_body_art" title="Henna">henna</a>. Their hairstyle was simple, the hair gathered back in tresses, often adorned at the ends with pearls and clusters of jewels. Women with slender waists were regarded as more attractive than those with larger figures. Women from the provinces and slaves pierced their left nostrils with rings, but well-born Persian women would not do this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most precious accessory for men was the <a href="/wiki/Turban" title="Turban">turban</a>. Although they lasted a long time it was necessary to have changes for different occasions like weddings and the <a href="/wiki/Nowruz" title="Nowruz">Nowruz</a>, while men of status never wore the same turban two days running. Clothes that became soiled in any way were changed immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turks_and_Tajiks">Turks and Tajiks</span></h3>
<p>Although the Safavid rulers and citizens were of native stock and continuously reasserted their Iranian identity, the power structure of the Safavid state was mainly divided into two groups: the Turkic-speaking military/ruling elite—whose job was to maintain the territorial integrity and continuity of the Iranian empire through their leadership—and the Persian-speaking administrative/governing elite—whose job was to oversee the operation and development of the nation and its identity through their high positions. Thus came the term "Turk and Tajik", which was used by native Iranians for many generations to describe the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persianate" title="Persianate">Persianate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Turko-Persian_tradition" title="Turko-Persian tradition">Turko-Persian</a>, nature of many dynasties which ruled over Greater Iran between the 12th and 20th centuries, in that these dynasties promoted and helped continue the dominant Persian linguistic and cultural identity of their states, although the dynasties themselves were of non-Persian (e.g. Turkic) linguistic origins. The relationship between the Turkic-speaking 'Turks' and Persian-speaking 'Tajiks' was symbiotic, yet some form of rivalry did exist between the two. As the former represented the "<i>people of the sword</i>" and the latter, "<i>the people of the pen</i>", high-level official posts would naturally be reserved for the Persians. Indeed, this had been the situation throughout Persian history, even before the Safavids, ever since the Arab conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Shah Tahmasp introduced a change to this, when he, and the other Safavid rulers who succeeded him, sought to blur the formerly defined lines between the two linguistic groups, by taking the sons of Turkic-speaking officers into the royal household for their education in the Persian language. Consequently, they were slowly able to take on administrative jobs in areas which had hitherto been the exclusive preserve of the ethnic Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_third_force:_Caucasians">The third force: Caucasians</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Daud_Khan_Undiladze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="272" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Daud_Khan_Undiladze.jpg/220px-Daud_Khan_Undiladze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Daud_Khan_Undiladze.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Daud_Khan_Undiladze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Daud_Khan_Undiladze" title="Daud Khan Undiladze">Daud Khan Undiladze</a>, military commander, <a href="/wiki/Ghilman" title="Ghilman">ghilman</a> and the governor of <a href="/wiki/Ganja,_Azerbaijan" title="Ganja, Azerbaijan">Ganja</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karabakh" title="Karabakh">Karabakh</a> from 1625 to 1630.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Georgians" title="Iranian Georgians">Iranian Georgians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Circassians" title="Iranian Circassians">Iranian Circassians</a></div>
